Abstract
This thesis focuses on understanding and modeling the mechanisms shaping the spatial structure of warming in the Tropical Pacific in response to climate change. The Tropical Pacific
Tropical Pacific, a key region both economically and ecologically, plays a crucial role in regulating global
global climate. Climate model projections predict marked and spatially contrasted warming in the coming decades. The spatial structure of this warming influences atmospheric stability
of the atmosphere, and partly determines changes in atmospheric and oceanic circulation and
and the response of ecosystems. It is therefore vital to identify the mechanisms that determine its evolution.
their evolution. However, the reliability of these projections is called into question by major discrepancies between models
between models, and between models and observations over recent decades. One of the causes put forward
to explain this diversity is the presence of strong systematic biases in climate models, particularly in the Tropical Pacific. In order to resolve these uncertainties, this work focuses on three major questions
around three major questions concerning the spatial structure of warming in the Tropical Pacific: (1) what are the mechanisms involved, (2) what are the causes of this inter-model diversity?
and (3) how do mean-state biases affect model projections?
To answer these questions, an original methodological approach is developed. The first
is based on a statistical analysis of simplified surface ocean heat balances applied to
to climate models in the CMIP database. The second is based on dedicated ocean simulations
to correct the influence of current climate model biases on future projections, and to quantify
as well as quantifying the individual contributions of changes in air-sea heat fluxes,
and freshwater fluxes to these projections.
The results reveal that changes in heat fluxes govern the meridional pattern of warming
warming, with greater warming at the equator and less in the southeast Pacific.
Changes in wind stress, for their part, control the distribution of warming along the equator
the equator, influencing zonal gradients in surface temperature and the intensity of trade winds. The
equatorial ocean response to changes in wind stress is also highly dependent on the current mean
which explains some of the inter-model scatter in this response. Projections
corrected for mean-state biases converge on a marked equatorial warming, a weakening of the east-west gradient at the equator and a relative cooling of the southeast Pacific. These results
underline that climate model biases are a major source of divergence between the spatial
the spatial structures of the warming they project, with the remainder of this dispersion being mainly due to the diversity of changes in the climate system.
to the diversity of changes in the intensity of projected trade winds. Finally, analysis of the recent period suggests that the contradictions between observed and modelled changes in zonal temperature gradients
are largely explained by internal variability, which is underestimated in climate models.
These results provide a solid basis for anticipating the impacts of climate change in the
Tropical Pacific. While this thesis has focused primarily on reducing uncertainties in physical parameter projections, preliminary results also point to more convincing projections for biota.
biogeochemistry and ecosystems of the Tropical Pacific Ocean.
Keywords: Climate change, Tropical Pacific, Climate models, Future projections,

Abstract
This thesis explores the mechanisms shaping the spatial pattern of tropical Pacific warming under
climate change, and how these are represented in climate models. The tropical Pacific plays a central
role in the global climate system and supports vital ecological and economic functions. Climate models
project substantial, heterogeneous warming in this region over the coming decades. The spatial structure
of this warming influences atmospheric stability and drives changes in atmospheric and oceanic circulation, with significant implications for ecosystems. Understanding the mechanisms that govern this
pattern is therefore crucial. However, the reliability of these projections is undermined by large discrepancies among climate models and between models and recent observations. A key reason for this spread
is the persistent systematic biases of climate models in the tropical Pacific. This thesis addresses these
uncertainties through three central questions: (1) What physical mechanisms shape the tropical Pacific
warming spatial structure? (2) What causes the differences among climate model projections? (3) How can recent
can recent observed trends be reconciled with model output? To address these, the thesis develops an
original two-pronged approach. First, it uses statistical analyses of simplified upper ocean heat budgets
applied to models from the CMIP database. Second, it employs targeted ocean simulations that isolate and quantify the individual roles of air-sea heat fluxes, wind stress, and freshwater forcing, while
minimizing the influence of present-day model biases. The findings show that changes in surface heat
fluxes largely control the meridional structure of warming, with amplified warming along the equator
and muted warming in the Southeast Pacific. Zonal patterns are shaped primarily by changes in wind
stress, which modulate both the east-west surface temperature gradient and trade wind strength. Crucially, the ocean's response to wind changes depends strongly on the background mean state, explaining
much of the inter-model spread. When future projections are corrected for mean state biases, they consistently show enhanced equatorial warming, a weakened east-west gradient, and relative cooling in the
Southeast Pacific. This highlights the dominant role of mean state biases in driving divergence among
models, particularly through their effect on projected trade wind changes. Finally, analysis of the recent
observational record suggests that much of the mismatch between observed and modeled zonal temperature trends can be attributed to internal variability, an element that is underrepresented in many models.
Altogether, this work lays a robust foundation for improving climate change projections in the tropical Pacific. While the thesis primarily focuses on physical parameters, preliminary results also suggest
improved model performance in projecting biogeochemical and ecosystem responses.
Keywords: Climate change, Tropical Pacific, Climate models, Future projections, Ocean-atmosphere
dynamics, Mean state bias
iii

1.1 Motivations

1.1
Motivations

[FIGURE 1.1] Average annual distribution (between 2005 and 2015) of tuna catches (Source: FAO. Figure: reproduced with permission from P. Taconnet (MARBEC)) Climate change is now a major global concern, with particularly pressing scientific, socio-economic and political issues at stake. In this global context, the Tropical Pacific appears to be a privileged study region, as much for its ecological richness as for its sensitivity to climatic variations. Home to nearly 25% of the world's marine biodiversity hotspots, the Tropical Pacific plays a central role in the preservation of ocean ecosystems (Payri et al. 2019). Fisheries, both artisanal and industrial, are an economic and social mainstay for many countries. For example, more than 50% of the world's tuna catches are made in the western and central tropical Pacific ( see Figure 1.1; Bell et al. 2021, Lehodey et al. 2021). However, the dynamics of these key resources are highly dependent on regional climate variability. The Tropical Pacific is indeed the scene of major climatic phenomena, such as ENSO (El Niño Southern Oscillation), which modulate the climate of riparian countries, but also that of more distant regions such as Brazil (Grimm et al. 2009; Rodrigues et al. 2017) or South Africa (Reason et al. 2002). The fishing economy in the Tropical Pacific is directly affected by these climatic variations. For example, during El Niño episodes, warm water masses in the western Pacific move eastwards, bringing with them a shift in tuna populations. Conversely, species accustomed to the colder waters off the Peruvian coast desert the region. La Niña episodes, characterized by an upwelling of cold waters in the eastern Pacific, have the opposite effect. This climatic variability significantly disrupts regional fisheries (Lehodey et al. 2021). In addition to interannual fluctuations, climate change is inducing profound transformations in the Tropical Pacific ocean system: rising surface water temperatures, extension of the Warm Pool (warm waters of the western Pacific), modification of ocean currents, intensification of ENSO events, increased ocean acidification (Cheng et al. 2019; Cai et al. 2021; Pörtner et al. 2022)... These physico-chemical changes directly threaten marine ecosystems and the stability of the region's fishery resources (Bell et al. 2021). This ecological vulnerability inevitably translates into socio-economic vulnerability, particularly marked for the many island countries in this region (see Figure 1.2, Bell et al. 2021). These territories, strongly

1.2 Climatology and variability of the Tropical Pacific in observations

[FIGURE 1.2] Tropical Pacific Islands. Source: Encyclopædia Britannica, Inc. dependent on natural resources, are among the most exposed to the impacts of climate change, such as rising sea levels and the intensification of tropical cyclones. Degradation of coral reefs, the mainstay of food fisheries and tourism, and changes in deep-sea fishing stocks (see Figure 1.1) directly threaten the livelihoods of local populations (Bell et al. 2011). Despite these crucial issues, our understanding of the Tropical Pacific's response to climate change remains limited. Current climate models present significant biases in the representation of the Tropical Pacific, notably concerning the representation of the equatorial cold water tongue, zonal currents and interannual variability. These uncertainties seriously limit our ability to reliably project the future evolution of this ocean. It is against this scientific and societal backdrop that this thesis is set. Its main objective is to improve our understanding of the future evolution of Tropical Pacific physics in the face of climate change. By refining our knowledge, this work aims to provide policy-makers and local communities with the tools they need to develop more effective adaptation strategies, thereby contributing to the resilience of island societies faced with the growing challenges of climate change. 1.2 Climatology and variability of the Tropical Pacific in observations 1.2.1 Climatology The climatology of the Tropical Pacific results from complex interactions between the atmosphere and the ocean, characterized by a strong zonal asymmetry along the equator. This asymmetry is mainly

[FIGURE 1.3] Walker circulation in the Pacific. Source: Thomson Higher Education Along the equator, the trade winds induce poleward Ekman transport (to the right of the wind in the northern hemisphere, and to the left in the southern hemisphere), favoring upwelling of subsurface water at the equator (Figure 1.5.d,e). However, surface waters show a strong zonal asymmetry. The western Pacific has particularly warm waters, forming the Warm Pool, with the highest surface temperatures on the planet (over 28°C), in contrast to the east, where waters are cooler (around 24°C, Figure 1.5.a). This asymmetry is explained by the Sverdrup balance: the trade winds exert a westward force on the ocean, counterbalanced by an eastward oceanic pressure force. This results in a thickening of the surface layer and a sinking of the thermocline to 150-200 m in the west of the basin, compared with around 50 m in the east (Figure 1.5.c). The Cold Tongue, a band of colder surface waters extending into the eastern and central equatorial Pacific, results from the upwelling of cold waters and the proximity of the thermocline (Figure 1.5.e). Inter-hemispheric asymmetry, notably the existence of the ITCZ north of the equator, intensifies the southern hemisphere trade winds, favoring coastal upwellings along the South American coasts and accentuating the cooling of waters in the southeastern Pacific (Figure 1.5.a). Tropical Pacific climatology is essentially the product of dynamic coupling between ocean and atmosphere. Indeed, deep atmospheric convection is only triggered above a critical surface temperature threshold of around 27.5°C (Sulochana et al. 1984; Graham et al. 1987). Deep convection therefore occurs mainly over the Warm Pool along the equator, while it is absent in the eastern Pacific. This east-west gradient of tropospheric heating generates a zonally asymmetric heat source, which drives the Walker circulation (see Figure 1.3). Theoretically described by Gill (Gill 1980), this circulation consists of an ascent of the air over the Warm Pool, an eastward shift in altitude, then a subsidence over the

1.2 Climatology and variability of the Tropical Pacific in observations

Pacific, and finally a return to the surface towards the west, strengthening the trade winds.
The oceanic dynamics of the Tropical Pacific are organized into several main structures (see the
zonal section in Figure 1.4). At the surface, between 4°N and 8°S, the South Equatorial Current (SEC)
Current (SEC) flows westwards (Wyrtki et al. 1984). This current is the result of geostrophic transport
associated with equatorial upwelling, which induces a minimum of surface pressure at the equator, generating
westward transport on both sides of the equator. It is particularly intense in its northern
equatorial branch. North of 10°N, the North Equatorial Current (NEC) also flows in a westerly direction.
also flows westwards, but with less intensity. Between these two currents, the North Equatorial Countercurrent (NECC), a narrow, intense current, flows eastwards
between 4°N and 10°N, in the opposite direction to the trade winds. This current is linked to the convergence of the trade winds and the upwelling
upwelling caused by the ITCZ, generating a northward pressure gradient.
In the subsurface, the Equatorial Undercurrent (EUC) flows eastwards beneath the
SEC, along the thermocline slope and gradually rising from west to east. As mentioned
previously, the trade winds induce a westward force that is offset by an opposite pressure gradient
pressure gradient over the thermocline. The EUC is directly linked to this zonal pressure gradient
pressure gradient, which is no longer balanced by the force exerted by the winds at depth.

[FIGURE 1.4] Tropical Pacific Ocean Circulation. Schematic diagram of horizontal and vertical circulation in the Tropical Pacific. (Philander 1989) The Tropical Pacific is also characterized by an important meridional circulation organized into subtropical cells (STC or Shallow tropical cells) (see meridional cross-section Figure 1.4 and Figure 1.5).

[FIGURE 1.5] .d,e). These STCs play a fundamental role in exchanges between subtropical and equatorial regions. They operate in three stages: surface waters subduct due to Ekman pumping in the heart of the subtropical gyres (20°-30° latitude), then advect towards the equator in the subsurface along isopycnes. These waters then join the EUC, rise to the surface thanks to equatorial upwelling, and are finally transported poleward by trade-wind-induced Ekman transport. This meridional circulation is linked to Ekman transport, which dominates at the surface, and geostrophic transport, which dominates beneath the Ekman layer. In fact, the trade winds that dominate the entire tropical band induce poleward Ekman transport, which intensifies near the equator due to variations in the Coriolis parameter. At the same time, the trade winds induce a deeper thermocline and a higher free surface in the west than in the east of the basin over much of the tropics. The resulting eastward pressure force is associated with equatorward geostrophic transport in both hemispheres. It is this inverted gradient that allows subducted waters to flow towards the equator and feed the EUC. These meridional circulation cells

play a crucial role in the thermal regulation of the equatorial Pacific, bringing colder waters
waters to the EUC and evacuating warm surface waters to the subtropics.

The Equatorial Pacific is particularly sensitive to this cycle, due to the annual meridional shift of the ITCZ. During the boreal spring (March-April), the ITCZ moves closer to the equator in response to increased solar heating. This migration leads to a significant weakening of the trade winds (see Figure 1.6.a), which reduces poleward Ekman transport, weakens equatorial upwelling and causes a deepening of the thermocline in the eastern Pacific. These processes lead to a warming of the ocean surface in this region (as illustrated in Figure 1.6.b). Conversely, between late summer and early boreal autumn (August6

Eastern and Western Equatorial Pacific. In the east, where the trade winds are particularly intense (Figure 1.5.a), the SST seasonal cycle is particularly strong.

and the thermocline much deeper, the seasonal variability of the SST is much more attenuated.
This seasonal modulation also affects equatorial currents. Counter-intuitively, the
Equatorial Undercurrent (EUC) intensifies as the trade winds weaken, reaching its maximum in the boreal spring
spring (May in the eastern Pacific, June in the central and western regions, Johnson et al.
2002; Stellema et al. 2022). At the same time, the Southern Equatorial Current (SEC) shows strong seasonal variability
seasonality, with a marked intensification during the austral winter (boreal summer) when the trade winds
stronger in the equatorial band (Chen et al. 2015). Finally, there is a seasonal asymmetry
in STCs, with differentiated intensification between the northern and southern hemispheres depending on the position of the
ITCZ. This modulation also varies according to longitude, with marked variations between
eastern and western regions of the basin (Reverdin et al. 1994).

Heat balance

Ocean-atmosphere heat exchange takes place via two main types of flux: the heat flux from the ocean to the atmosphere and the heat flux from the atmosphere to the ocean.

(including short-wave solar radiation and long-wave infrared radiation) and
long-wave infrared radiation) and turbulent flows (of latent and sensible heat), shown in Figure 1.7 and

[FIGURE 1.8] . Solar radiation is the main source of energy for the ocean. The Tropical Pacific region receives an intense solar input due to its proximity to the equator (Figure 1.7 and

[FIGURE 1.8] .a). As the albedo of the ocean surface is low ( 0.05 to 0.1), around 90% of incident solar energy is absorbed in the first 20 to 30 meters for oligotrophic waters, warming the surface layers of the ocean (Wild et al. 2013). However, this solar radiation is less intense in regions of deep convection such as the ITCZ and SPCZ, due to the extensive cloud cover that reflects solar radiation.

[FIGURE 1.8] Radiation budget and energy flux in the Tropical Pacific (averages 1999-2019). Main components: (a) Net solar flux (SW), (b) Net longwave (LW) balance, (c) LW upwelling radiation, (d) LW downwelling radiation, (e) Latent heat flux, (f) Total net flux. Values in W/m2, red = energy gain, blue = energy loss. In addition, greenhouse gases in the atmosphere (water vapor, CO2, CH4, etc.) emit infrared radiation in accordance with Stefan-Boltzmann's black-body law. A fraction of this infrared radiation is absorbed by the ocean surface and warms it: this is the longwave downwelling radiation (Figure 1.8.d). This flow is amplified in cloudy regions, as the clouds effectively re-emit the infrared radiation towards the surface. Downward infrared heating is therefore greatest in areas of active convection (ITCZ, SPCZ) and in the Cold Tongue, where low stratus clouds predominate. Solar radiation (Figure 1.8.a) and downward infrared radiation (Figure 1.8.d) warm the ocean surface. The ocean cools by emitting rising infrared radiation. This flux is greatest in regions with the warmest waters, such as the Warm Pool (Figure 1.8.c).

The ocean also cools through turbulent flows. The latent heat flux corresponds
the energy required to evaporate the ocean's surface water. This process depends on wind speed
and the specific humidity gradient between ocean and atmosphere (Yu 2007). Evaporation is
particularly intense in the warmer regions of the Warm Pool (Figure 1.8.e) and more limited in the
the Cold Tongue, where temperatures are lower. This asymmetry is governed by Clausius-Clapeyron's law: at constant humidity, evaporation capacity increases with surface temperature. This
This phenomenon, known as evaporative cooling, acts as a negative feedback limiting the excessive warming of surface waters. However, this process is less effective near the equator
where wind speeds are generally lower than at subtropical latitudes. Sensible heat flux
heat exchange (amplified by atmospheric turbulence), also contributes to cooling.
turbulence), also contributes to ocean cooling, although its role remains secondary
to evaporation. The net heat flux resulting from all these exchanges leads to a net warming in cold waters.
warming in the cold waters of the Cold Tongue and southeastern Tropical Pacific, and net cooling in the warm waters of the Warm Tongue.
net cooling of the warm waters of the Warm Pool and inter-tropical convergence zones (Figure 1.8.f). On long time scales, these net heat fluxes at the ocean-atmosphere interface are offset

2010). For example, STCs export excess heat from the cold-water tongue to the subtropics

1.2.4
Internal climate variability in the Tropical Pacific
1.2.4.1
The ENSO phenomenon
The Tropical Pacific is home to the main climatic fluctuation that dominates global climate variations
climate on interannual time scales: El Niño/La Niña (ENSO, El Niño Southern Oscillation)
Oscillation) (Timmermann et al. 2018,McPhaden et al. 2020). ENSO affects global meteorology
with major impacts on precipitation, temperature and extreme events. Every 2 to 7
years, a weakening of the trade winds reduces the zonal pressure gradient and equatorial wind stress.
This allows the warm waters of the Warm Pool to migrate towards the eastern Equatorial Pacific. Usually, the trade winds blow from the eastern Pacific up to 160°E, but during El Niño, their weakening
extends to around 160°W, shifting the Warm Pool eastwards by around 40° of longitude. This
weakening also leads to a deepening of the thermocline in the central and eastern parts of the
Equatorial Pacific, an upwelling of the thermocline in the west and the transport of warm waters
from west to east. This eastward advection of the Warm Pool brings with it the displacement of deep convection cells
generating intense rainfall along the west coast of South America and severe drought
severe droughts in Australia and the maritime continent (Figure 1.9).
The Bjerknes ocean-atmosphere positive feedback mechanism (Bjerknes 1969) governs the development of these El Niño events. It can be summarized as follows: an initial westerly wind anomaly
anomaly in the western Pacific moves the Warm Pool eastwards by zonal advection
zonal advection, warming surface waters in the central Pacific. This westerly wind anomaly also generates
downwelling Kelvin wave, which deepens the thermocline and warms the surface waters
waters in this region (Lengaigne et al. 2002). As the SST in this region is close to the critical threshold
threshold for triggering convection (around 27.5°C), a slight thermal anomaly may be enough to trigger
trigger convection in this region, reinforcing the westerly wind anomalies, in line with the theoretical mechanism proposed by Gill (Gill 1980). This anomaly reinforces the initial
warming. In this way, a positive feedback is initiated: surface temperature and wind anomalies reinforce each other
reinforce each other (Figure 1.10).

1.2 Climatology and variability of the Tropical Pacific in observations

[FIGURE 1.9] Typical ENSO phases in the Equatorial Pacific, with diagrams showing neutral, El Niño and La Niña conditions in the Equatorial Pacific. Source: Australian Bureau of Meteorology. As El Niño progresses, heat content anomalies develop: accumulation in the east, depletion in the west. This generates poleward Sverdrup transport, which progressively reduces the heat content of the equatorial band, eventually bringing the El Niño event to an end, and sometimes favoring a transition to La Niña, the opposite phase to El Niño. This corresponds to a strengthening of normal conditions, with negative temperature anomalies in the Equatorial Pacific, a Warm Pool and convection zone confined to the extreme west of the Equatorial Pacific by the strengthening trade winds, and a rising thermocline to the east and deepening to the west (Figure 1.10). This recharge-discharge mechanism, combining positive Bjerknes feedback and negative feedback delayed by slow ocean dynamics, is known as the recharged oscillator theory (e.g. Jin et al. 2020). The alternation of these warm and cold phases constitutes the ENSO (El Niño/Southern Oscillation) cycle, a coupling between the ocean (El Niño) and the atmosphere (Southern Oscillation). Beyond ENSO, the Pacific also exhibits interdecadal variability, mainly due to11

1.2 Climatology and variability of the Tropical Pacific in observations

[FIGURE 1.10] Bjerknes ocean-atmosphere feedbackExplanatory diagram of the Bjerknes feedback. Source : Puy Martin 2016

[FIGURE 1.11] Decadal variability observed in the Tropical Pacific. (A) Linear trend in annual SST (1992-2009, colors) and sea level anomalies (black contours). (B) Low-pass filtered SST variance at 8 yr. (C) Band-pass SST variance 8-40 yr. (D) Low-pass filtered SST time series over Niño 3.4, western Tropical Pacific, and globe regions. Adapted from Power et al. 2021. undermined by the Interdecadal Pacific Oscillation (IPO). The IPO is characterized by changes in ocean surface temperature on time scales of 20 to 30 years, much longer than ENSO cycles (Power et al. 1999) (Figure 1.11.d). The IPO has a similar but more extensive structure than ENSO, with a particularly pronounced footprint in the North Pacific (Figure 1.11.C). IPO contributes more to SST fluctuations in tropical regions off the equator, contributing up to 40% of the total variance in SST anomalies, while ENSO remains the major contributor to SST variability at the equator. IPO affects precipitation, temperature, marine ecosystems and fisheries productivity on a Pacific scale (Mantua et al. 1997). It is likely that the IPO partially reflects the low-frequency component of ENSO on decadal scales and the response to ENSO teleconnections in the extratropics. However, the debate remains open as to whether the IPO is able to modulate ENSO's properties (intensity, frequency) according to its warm or cold phase (e.g. Capotondi et al. 2023).

1.2.4.2
Long-term trends in observations
Observations show an increase in global average temperature over the 20th century.
century Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) 2023. This trend is not monotonic, however, and has been interrupted by periods of little warming, or even temporary cooling
(Figure 1.12, top left). Between 2001 and 2013, a marked slowdown in global surface warming
warming, often referred to as a hiatus, has given rise to intense debate as to its origins and implications
on the understanding of global climate change (Easterling et al. 2009; Fyfe et al. 2013). These
hiatus periods, such as those of 1940-1975 and 2001-2013, coincide with negative phases of the
the IPO (Figure 1.12, top left), suggesting an important role for natural Pacific variability
in modulating climate trends (Kosaka et al. 2013; Meehl et al. 2013; Lee et al. 2022).
Thus, IPO can temporarily mask underlying anthropogenic warming (Meehl et al. 2016),
complicating the interpretation of climate trends observed over relatively short periods
(less than 40 years). During the negative phases of the IPO, there is a significant strengthening of the
Hadley and Walker cells, leading to an intensification of the trade winds (England et al. 2014). In response to these atmospheric changes, the SST cools in the central and eastern tropical Pacific
central and eastern tropical Pacific (Figure 1.12, top right), via the increase in Ekman divergence
divergence and the intensification of equatorial upwelling, as well as an acceleration of subtropical gyres. One of the major
major oceanic consequences of this intensification of the trade winds is the acceleration of shallow circulation cells in the Pacific Ocean (Figure 1.12, bottom).
cold-water upwelling at the equator and warm-water subduction in the subtropics.
This reorganization of oceanic circulation redistributes heat vertically and horizontally, favoring the accumulation of heat in the subsurface.
heat accumulation in the Pacific subsurface, while cooling the surface layer
(Balmaseda et al. 2013). So, during these hiatus phases, the ocean continues to store heat,
but this heat is transferred at depth, limiting its immediate impact on surface temperature
(Trenberth et al. 2013). Although some uncertainties remain about the exact magnitude of the strengthening
of the east-west SST gradient observed over recent decades, depending on the datasets used
used (Figure 1.13), all observations converge on the strengthening of the zonal
SST and the intensification of trade winds over periods ranging from the last 4 decades (e.g. Wills et al.
2022) to the last century (e.g. Gopika et al. 2024). This convergence between different data sources reinforces confidence in the existence of a "La Niña"-type response in the Tropical Pacific
during the recent period (Coats et al. 2017; Lee et al. 2022).

1.3. The Tropical Pacific in climate models

1.3
The Tropical Pacific in climate models
In the previous section, we described the observed mean state of the Tropical Pacific and its natural variability.
variability, revealing a complex coupled ocean-atmosphere system. Given the major socio-economic stakes in the region, it is vital to understand how this critical zone will evolve over the coming decades in the face of the challenges posed by climate change. Today, climate models are the most sophisticated tools available to the scientific community for exploring
to explore the future evolution of the climate system. They enable us to simulate a wide range of scenarios
scenarios and anticipate potential changes with ever finer spatial and temporal resolutions.
resolutions. Their ability to reproduce the main characteristics of the Tropical Pacific, as well as to
future changes, is a considerable asset when it comes to analyzing anthropogenic influences on
present and future climate. However, despite their constant progress, climate models still present
structural biases that limit the accuracy of their projections, particularly in tropical regions.
tropical regions. This section summarizes the ability of climate models to represent the current
Pacific and how they project its future evolution under the effect of climate change.
climate change.
1.3.1
CMIP climate models
1.3.1.1
Climate models
A climate model is a numerical tool used to simulate the behavior of the atmosphere,
oceans, continental surfaces, the biosphere and the cryosphere. The physical components
components of these models are called general circulation models (GCMs). These
are based on the numerical integration of the fundamental physical equations governing these
fluid envelopes. These equations are discretized on a three-dimensional grid (X, Y and Z). The equations governing the exchange of energy, mass and momentum are calculated at each point in an iterative
point iteratively from one time step to the next, producing long time series (Figure 1.14). This method makes it possible to represent climate dynamics on large spatial and temporal scales.
temporal scale, with the size of the grid cells defining the model's "resolution". The spatial resolution
of GCMs, defined by the size of the grid cells, is limited by the high computational costs
associated with this type of global modeling. Many important but fine-scale spatial phenomena
These include boundary layer turbulence, convection, cloud microphysics and radiative transfer. These GCMs are being developed by various institutions worldwide
mainly climate research centers, universities, national meteorological agencies and government laboratories. To coordinate the global modeling effort and facilitate inter-model comparisons, the World Climate Research Programme (WCRP)
set up the Coupled Model Intercomparison Project (CMIP, Meehl et al. 2000) in 1995. The main aim of CMIP is twofold: to facilitate systematic evaluation of climate model performance
models and to understand model discrepancies in order to improve the robustness of climate
projections (Taylor et al. 2012). The current phase, CMIP6, was launched as part of the sixth cycle
of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC, Eyring et al. 2016). Simulations from CMIP6 were thus used as the basis for the climate results presented in the sixth
Assessment Report (AR6), published in 2021/2022. In particular, CMIP analyses make it possible to
assess the fidelity of models in reproducing the current climate, climate responses to natural and anthropogenic forcings, and the uncertainties associated with future projections in a variety of emission scenarios
emission scenarios (O'Neill et al. 2016).

1.3. The Tropical Pacific in climate models

[FIGURE 1.14] Digitization of the Earth system in a general circulation model (GCM). Source: NOAA. 1.3.1.2 The CMIP project

[FIGURE 1.15] CMIP project themes. Different research themes of the CMIP project (Eyring et al. 2016). During the fifth phase of the CMIP project (CMIP5), around 50 GCMs models, developed by 20 climate modeling centers, participated. This number has increased considerably with the sixth phase of the project (CMIP6), which brings together nearly 70 models from 33 institutions around the world. CMIP is organized into several thematic programs (Figure 1.15, Eyring et al. 2016), each targeting specific aspects of the climate system. The Decadal Climate Prediction Project provides short-term climate projections, on a 10-30 year, decadal scale, paramount for medium-term planning and adaptation to climate change. In parallel, the Coupled Climate Carbon Cycle Model Intercomparison Project focuses on the dynamics and interactions within the carbon cycle, while Ice Sheet Model Intercomparison

Project examines the interactions between oceans, land and ice. In this thesis, the
historical simulations and Scenario Model Intercomparison Project. The historical simulations component simulates climate evolution from 1850 to the present day, integrating
natural and anthropogenic forcings, enabling us to assess the models' ability to reproduce past
past climate. The Scenario Model Intercomparison Project component provides future climate projections
climate in response to different future emissions scenarios.
1.3.1.3
IPCC scenarios
The IPCC scenarios provide possible evolutions of different climate forcings, such as
such as changes in greenhouse gases, aerosols, ozone, continental surfaces, etc. Given that future
future anthropogenic emissions cannot be known with certainty, the IPCC has developed several
emission trajectories to frame the uncertainties. In the previous CMIP exercise (CMIP5),
four scenarios called Representative Concentration Pathways (RCPs) were used. These scenarios
define different future levels of radiative forcing (expressed in W/m2 ) by 2100 compared to the
pre-industrial era, corresponding to different socio-economic and climate policy assumptions
(Figure 1.16).

[FIGURE 1.16] Projected annual CO2 emissions in CMIP6 SSP scenarios. Source: IPCC, AR6 Report (Masson-Delmotte et al. 2023) RCP2.6 is an optimistic scenario for an additional radiative forcing of 2.6 W/m2, assuming strong emission limitation to keep warming below 2°C. The scenarios RCP4.5 (4.5 W/m2 additional) and RCP6.0 (6 W/m2 additional) are intermediate scenarios with a gradual stabilization of GHG concentrations. RCP8.5 (8.5 W/m2 additional) is the most pessimistic scenario, projecting a continuous increase in emissions without active climate policies. Since the IPCC's Sixth Assessment Report (AR6), future scenarios have been supplemented by Shared Socioeconomic Pathways (SSPs), which describe five possible socioeconomic trajectories. These describe a range of possibilities, from sustainable development scenarios with a high priority on reducing inequalities and protecting the environment (SSP1) to scenarios marked by regional rivalry and limited international cooperation (SSP3). There are also futures with growing inequalities (SSP4) and rapid development based on fossil fuels (SSP5). The combination of SSPs and RCPs in CMIP6 models enables a wide range of climate futures to be explored, assessing potential impacts on temperature, precipitation and extreme events. Among them, SSP585 is a scenario combining SSP5 (rapid fossil-fuel-based development) with RCP8.5 (a scenario

emissions). It represents a future characterized by a continuous increase in
emissions and significant global warming by the end of the century, with no meaningful
climate policies. Although the current consensus is that observed trajectories are closer to the
closer to the SSP3-7.0 scenario, in this work, for reasons of time constraints and storage capacity
only the most pessimistic scenarios have been analyzed: RCP 8.5 for CMIP5 and
SSP 5-8.5 for CMIP6. What's more, these 8.5 scenarios present large-scale changes,
thus reducing the influence of noise associated with internal climate variability on the structures
projected over the 21st century.
1.3.1.4
Global physical projections

[FIGURE 1.17] Global physical projections in SSP5-8.5. Global physical projections from CMIP6 models under the SSP5-8.5 scenario to 2100. (a) Global surface temperature, (b) extent of Arctic sea ice, (c) ocean acidification (mean pH), (d) rise in mean sea level. Source: IPCC, AR6 (Aigner et al. 2023) Physical projections from CMIP6 models under the SSP585 scenario reveal significant changes by the end of the 21st century. Overall, the models agree on several major evolutions of the climate system, including global ocean warming, with an estimated mean SST increase of 3.78°C (±1.03°C) as illustrated in Figure 1.17 (Kwiatkowski et al. 2020) ( and a sea level rise of 0.63 to 1.01 m by 2100. These global physical changes are accompanied by regionally differentiated responses, with, for example, more pronounced warming in the Arctic (±5 ◦C). On a regional scale, the dispersion between the projections of different models becomes more marked. This diversity is explained by differing climate sensitivity between models (Zelinka et al. 2020), variations in parameterizations of climate feedbacks (Meehl et al. 2020), as well as the impact of model resolution (Roberts et al. 2020), among other factors.

1.3.2
The Tropical Pacific and climate change
1.3.2.1
Climate model projections
At the regional scale of the Tropical Pacific, climate models simulate changes in temperature
precipitation, winds, currents and the natural variability of regional climate
of regional climate (Cai et al. 2021). Climate models project widespread warming in the Tropical
Tropical Pacific, particularly pronounced in the equatorial band (Collins et al. 2010; Liu et al.
2005; Xie et al. 2010). The SST could increase by up to 5°C compared with pre-industrial levels
in certain regions of the Equatorial Pacific (Cai et al. 2015, Figure 1.19.a). This warming is generally
is generally more pronounced in the east than in the west of the basin in most models, a warming pattern
often referred to as "El Niño-like warming". This weakening of the zonal temperature gradient
gradient along the equator is linked to the weakening of the Walker cell (Liu et al. 2005;
Vecchi et al. 2007; Xie et al. 2010; Cai et al. 2015; Plesca et al. 2018). Section 1.3.2.1 summarizes
the resulting projected changes, with a decrease in the intensity of trade winds and equatorial currents

of the eastern equatorial Pacific compared with surrounding regions (Vecchi et al. 2007;
Zhang et al. 2014), a flattening of the thermocline (black curve for the current thermocline, red
for projections), more pronounced warming at the surface than at depth (Cai et al. 2021), and
an increase in precipitation across the equatorial band (Chadwick et al. 2013;

[FIGURE 1.18] Future greenhouse-induced changes at the surface (shown only for the North Pacific) and in the upper ocean along the equator, as well as in meridional cross-sections. Greenhouse-induced changes (red arrows) on mean Walker circulation (dashed black arrows) and mean ocean currents (cyan arrows) are shown. (Cai et al. 2015) To better understand precipitation changes, the concept of Relative Sea Surface Temperature (RSST) is useful (Xie et al. 2010). RSST, defined as the difference between the local SST and its average over the entire tropical band (25°S-25°N), highlights regions that are warming more or less than the tropical average.

1.3. The Tropical Pacific in climate models

On the one hand, this highlights the spatial structuring of warming (Xie et al. 2010; Watanabe
et al. 2021), with positive RSST anomalies in the central and eastern equatorial Pacific, and negative
negative anomalies in the subtropical regions of the Southern Hemisphere (Figure 1.19.b). RSST
is also a very good proxy for changes in atmospheric stability and precipitation.
tropical upper troposphere tends to warm uniformly with global warming,
variations in atmospheric stability are mainly linked to RSST anomalies. A positive
positive RSST anomaly will tend to increase atmospheric convection, and thus intensify
precipitation (Izumo et al. 2020). Conversely, a negative RSST anomaly stabilizes the atmospheric layer
inhibiting precipitation, which explains the consistency of sign between changes in
RSST and rainfall (1.19.b and 1.19.c).
Beyond the average changes described above, natural climate variability, and in particular the ENSO phenomenon, is also impacted by climate change in models (Cai
et al. 2021). The latter tend to robustly simulate an intensification of extreme El
Niño events (Cai et al. 2021), with an estimated doubling of their occurrence: from one event every
20 years (1890-1990) to around one event every 10 years by the end of the 21st century (Cai et al. 2021;
Cai et al. 2014; Yun et al. 2021). This intensification is explained by increased stratification of the surface layers
Equatorial Pacific. This is the result of more intense warming at the surface than in the
surface salinity due to increased precipitation. The
mixing layer being more stratified, its response to wind forcing is amplified, reinforcing the likelihood of extreme El Niño events (Cai et al. 2018; Carréric et al. 2020). Despite these results, significant
uncertainties remain regarding the evolution of ENSO events in the future (Cai et al. 2021), due to
climate model biases, but also the disparity of ENSO-related mechanisms in the models
and the high internal variability of the climate system, which may mask anthropogenic trends
on short to intermediate timescales. These uncertainties require us to continue refining our understanding
understanding of tropical climate processes and to improve numerical modeling of the Tropical Pacific for more robust projections.
1.3.2.2
Relative cooling in the Southeast Pacific
The Southeast Pacific is showing less warming than the rest of the Tropical Pacific,
a phenomenon present in both climate models and observations (Gopika et al. 2024).
This relative cooling is consistently reproduced by the majority of climate simulations
(see Figure 1.19), in agreement with observational data, giving it remarkable robustness (Seager et al. 2019; Chung et al. 2024). The intensification of trade winds in this region, induced by
global warming, could be the main driver of this relative cooling, as it increases surface evaporation and latent heat transfer from the ocean to the atmosphere (Xie et al.
2010; Power et al. 2021). This intensification of the trade winds is closely linked to the poleward expansion
of the Hadley cell in the Southern Hemisphere (Grise et al. 2019; Jebri et al. 2020), which shifts subtropical high
subtropical high pressure towards higher latitudes, reinforcing the pressure gradients at the origin of the
southeast Pacific trade winds. This effect is amplified by the WES feedback (Wind-Evaporation-SST, Liu et al.
al. 2005; Xie et al. 2010), where temperature anomalies modify surface winds, influencing
evaporation and thus reinforcing regional thermal contrasts. At the same time, an increase
stratocumulus cover is observed in this region, linked to the negative RSST anomalies
stabilizing the atmosphere (Lin et al. 2014). These low, high-albedo clouds reflect more solar radiation, limiting heat absorption and helping to maintain a
relative cooling (Lin et al. 2014; Seager et al. 2019). Finally, teleconnections with the Southern Ocean could also contribute to this relative cooling. The relative cooling of the Southern Ocean
could indeed be advected by the meridional oceanic circulation towards the South-East Pacific via the
(Dong et al. 2022; Kang et al. 2023).

Points: robustness of projections (≥75% of models agreeing for a, b, d; ≥60% for c). 1.3.2.3 Increased warming at the equator While the role of the mechanisms involved in the relative cooling of the southeast Pacific is well established and the subject of broad scientific consensus, the nature of future equatorial warming and the associated processes remain far more uncertain. While the majority of models project an El Niño-type future state (weakening of the zonal gradient of equatorial SST and trade winds), some suggest, on the contrary, a strengthening of the zonal gradient and trade winds, characteristic of a La Niña-type warming. A summary of the main mechanisms favoring one or other of these scenarios is presented below, based on Figure 1.20. These processes are summarized in Table 1.1. Mechanisms favoring El Niño-type warming Several fundamental physical processes can explain the majority trend towards a weakening of the zonal gradient in the projections. Differential evaporative damping is one of the key processes that can lead to a weakening of the zonal temperature gradient.

1.3. The Tropical Pacific in climate models

[FIGURE 1.20] Physical mechanisms associated with equatorial warming of the El Niño or La Niña type. The three panels illustrate: (A) the effects of atmospheric stability and evaporative cooling, (B) the role of the ocean thermostat and upwelling, and (C) radiative feedbacks and water vapor. Left: pre-industrial climate; right: response to global warming. Source: Lee et al. 2022. (Knutson et al. 1995; Xie et al. 2010; Zhang et al. 2014). Due to the exponential relationship between temperature and saturation vapor pressure, evaporative cooling is much more efficient in the Warm Pool (where the climatological surface temperature is higher) than in the Cold Tongue. Thus, for the same radiative forcing, the evaporative response will be stronger in the Warm Pool than in the Cold Tongue, reducing the zonal SST gradient (Held et al. 2006) (see Figure 1.20.A). The intensity of climatological winds is also weaker at the equator than in the subtropics, also inducing weaker evaporative feedback, which may also explain the increased warming at the equator (Seager et al. 2003), as illustrated in Figure 1.23.a . In parallel with this fundamental thermodynamic process, other atmospheric mechanisms

also contribute to this weakening of the gradient. Indeed, on a global scale, tropical precipitation P is attributable to the export of moisture from the atmospheric boundary layer q to the
middle troposphere by the intensity of vertical circulation M (P ∼Mq, i.e. ∆P/P = ∆M/M +∆q/q
for future changes noted ∆). However, with global warming, atmospheric water vapour content
Clausius-Clapeyron (∆q/q ∼7% per degree). Tropical precipitation, on the other hand, cannot increase so quickly due to energy constraints. At the

radiative cooling in clear-sky regions. However, the black body law limits the increase
radiative cooling of the atmosphere: precipitation therefore only increases by around 2-3% per degree of warming
per degree of warming in climate models. This disparity implies a reduction in convective
mass fluxes ∆M/M of the order of -5%/K, leading to a weakening of the Walker circulation
Walker circulation (Held et al. 2006; Chou et al. 2004; Kang et al. 2023), a key process in the development
El Niño-type warming (Vecchi et al. 2007). Indeed, this slowdown reduces the intensity of the
equatorial trade winds, which limits the transport of warm water to the west and the upwelling of cold water to the east.
The reduction in upwelling leads to a deepening of the thermocline in the eastern Pacific,
reducing cold-water upwelling and promoting surface warming (Collins et al. 2010). This
mechanism leads to greater warming in the east than in the west, attenuating the zonal temperature gradient
temperature gradient and creating mean El Niño-like conditions (DiNezio et al. 2009).
Radiative processes can also reinforce El Niño-like warming (Figure
1.20.C). Warming of the ocean surface in the Warm Pool leads to intensified deep convection
deep convection and an increase in associated convective clouds, thus reducing solar radiation reaching the surface (Erfani et al. 2019). Conversely, the warming of the waters of the Cold
Tongue decreases the stability of the lower atmosphere and reduces stratocumulus cloud cover, lowering albedo in the region and increasing heat absorption via solar radiation
(Ramanathan et al. 1991; Cai et al. 2015; Stuecker et al. 2020). The effect of this feedback, positive in the east
east and negative in the west, leads to a reduction in the zonal SST gradient (Figure 1.20, panel C).
One study estimates this contribution at 20% of the zonal gradient reduction in a climate model
(Fu et al. 2023).
Other mechanisms linked to differential warming between oceans and continents (Tokinaga et al.
2012b) or the stabilization of the ocean by increased rainfall in the equatorial band (Kim et al. 2023)
could also contribute to increased warming in the eastern Pacific, but we won't go into detail
here.

Positive feedbacks
The preceding mechanisms describe factors likely to cause SST in the Equatorial Pacific.
In this section, I will discuss the positive feedback mechanisms that are likely to amplify a change in this gradient.

Bjerknes feedback (Bjerknes 1969) plays a central role in the coupled ocean-atmosphere dynamics of the Tropical Pacific.
Pacific. It is this feedback that enables the initial development of an ENSO event in boreal summer and autumn,
by amplifying the temperature anomalies (positive or negative) present at the start of summer. This
feedback can therefore amplify changes in zonal gradients of equatorial SST, whether El Niño or La Niña.

Lack of consensus
Despite decades of research, there is no clear consensus on the dominant mechanisms governing the Equatorial Pacific's response to global warming. Numerous studies
studies have approached the question from a variety of angles, mobilizing distinct methodologies. Some are based on statistical analyses of model ensembles and observations (Xie et al.
al. 2010; Zhang et al. 2014; Song et al. 2014; Ying et al. 2015; Coats et al. 2017; Seager et al. 2019;
Watanabe et al. 2024), while others take a more mechanistic approach, exploring physical processes via targeted simulations (Knutson et al. 1995; DiNezio et al. 2009; Merlis et al.
2011; Heede et al. 2021b; Li et al. 2023; Fu et al. 2023). The conclusions drawn from these two approaches
diverge significantly. Statistical approaches generally identify processes linked to surface fluxes
as dominant, be it cloud feedbacks on solar radiation (Ramanathan et al. 1991) or differential evaporative damping (Xie et al. 2010; Zhang et al. 2014),
while dynamic approaches emphasize the importance of the ocean's dynamic response via the
ocean thermostat, particularly on short time scales, or Bjerknes feedback. This heterogeneity of results largely reflects the inherent limitations of the methodologies employed, as well as the sensitivity of the conclusions to the models used.
as well as the sensitivity of conclusions to the models used, their configuration, or the period studied. It
the difficulty of prioritizing all the mechanisms at play in a system as complex as the coupled
as the coupled system of the Tropical Pacific.
1.3.3
Discrepancies between climate models and recent observations
As presented in section 1.2.4.2, observations over recent decades reveal a significant strengthening of the zonal SST gradient in the equatorial Pacific (Figure 1.21). This strengthening
is accompanied by an intensification of the trade winds and Walker circulation from the 1980s onwards
(Karnauskas et al. 2009; McGregor et al. 2014; Heede et al. 2023; Bai et al. 2023).
contrasts sharply with the majority of climate models, which simulate a weakening
SST gradient over the course of the 20th century (Coats et al. 2017; Lee et al. 2022). As illustrated by the

[FIGURE 1.21] , most CMIP models fail to reproduce the observed strengthening of the zonal SST gradient between 1981 and 2010, and more than half even simulate an opposite trend (Seager et al. 2022; Bai et al. 2023). This divergence is not confined to the temporal evolution of the zonal gradient (Figure 1.21). It can also be seen in the spatial structure of decadal variability in the Tropical Pacific.

This divergence can be seen not only in the temporal evolution of the zonal gradient (Figure 1.21), but also in the spatial structure of decadal variability in the Tropical Pacific (Figure 1.22). Potential causes of this discrepancy Two main hypotheses have been put forward to explain this discrepancy between models and observations: internal variability and model biases. The internal variability of the climate system, in particular the IPO, could explain this model-observation discrepancy. The recent transition from a positive to a negative phase of the IPO (1980-2020) may have strengthened the zonal SST gradient and the Walker circulation (Chung et al. 2019). However, models quasi-systematically underestimate the amplitude of this variability, particularly the SST fluctuations associated with the IPO and their link with ENSO, thus limiting their ability to fully reproduce the extent of the strengthening

However, several studies (Wills et al. 2022; Lee et al. 2022; Bai et al. 2023) suggest that even with a more accurate representation of internal variability, this mechanism cannot be the sole explanation for the observed discrepancies, as the amplitude of simulated decadal fluctuations is significantly lower than that observed in observations (Wills et al. 2022). Mean-state biases in climate models could also contribute to this divergence. They modify the relative intensity of the mechanisms responsible for strengthening or weakening the zonal SST gradient (Seager et al. 2019). These biases include insufficient cooling by upwelling and excessive westward extension of the cold water tongue (Bai et al. 2023). These biases

1.3.4
Impact of model biases on the reliability of projections
1.3.4.1
Climate model biases
This section details the systematic biases of climate models in the Tropical Pacific and
their implications. The best-known of these is the cold-water tongue bias in the
Tropical Pacific. Climate models tend to overestimate the intensity of equatorial upwellings
and their westward extension, resulting in a colder SST along the equatorial band
and a split Warm Pool, visible in Figure 1.23.c (Zheng et al. 2012 Li et al. 2016). This
marked cold-water tongue is also associated with overly strong trade winds and a dry bias in the
Pacific. The origin of this coupled ocean-atmosphere bias is unclear (Mechoso
et al. 1995; Guilyardi et al. 2009). Despite progress between CMIP5 and CMIP6, this bias persists in most models
majority of models (Planton et al. 2021).

[FIGURE 1.23] Average climate bias in the Tropical Pacific (1958-2019): (a) CMIP multi-model mean SST (°C), (b) multi-model mean precipitation (mm/day), (c) SST bias between CMIP and COBE, (d) precipitation bias between CMIP and JRA55. Conversely, CMIP models tend to overestimate SST in the extreme eastern Tropical Pacific, particularly near the South American coast (Figure 1.23.c). This overestimation could, among other things, be due to an underestimation of stratocumulus coverage in this region, leading to an underestimation of cloud albedo and thus an excess of solar radiation absorbed by the ocean (Mechoso et al. 2016; Zuidema et al. 2016). Finally, another major bias is that of the double ITCZ. The SPCZ simulated by models in the southern hemisphere is indeed too zonal and extends as far as the eastern Pacific, making it similar to the ITCZ in the northern hemisphere (Figure 1.23.d). This bias is mainly due to

attributed to thermodynamic processes linked to positive SST anomalies in the South-East Pacific.

Impact of biases on Tropical Pacific projections
As explained in section 1.3.3, the discrepancy between models and recent observations concerning the evolution of the zonal SST gradient and the Walker circulation can be partly attributed to the model biases described above.
model biases described above. Among these biases, those affecting the equatorial cold water tongue (Zhou et al. 2015; Seager et al. 2019) play a prominent role, as does the cloud-radiation feedback (Ying et al. 2019).

tropical ocean basins (McGregor et al. 2014). CMIP models systematically underestimate the amplitude of decadal variability in the Tropical Pacific and its relationship with ENSO, as illustrated in Fig.
1.22 (Power et al. 2021). This directly affects their ability to simulate changes in the Walker circulation, and in particular its recent intensification. This underestimation could be due to the

Methods for improving projections
In an attempt to reduce these uncertainties, the emerging constraints method has been developed.
It is based on the establishment of empirical relationships between observable climate variables and the future climate response simulated by the models.
future climate response simulated by the models (Hall et al. 2019, Eyring et al. 2019, Schlund et al.
2020). This method has been applied on several occasions in the Tropical Pacific region to better
constrain physical projections. For example, Brown et al. 2013 demonstrated that biases in the
influence future projections of temperature and precipitation in the western Tropical Pacific.
Western Tropical Pacific. Li et al. 2016 established that biases in the simulation of precipitation
in the western Tropical Pacific significantly affect SST projections. After statistical
of these biases via the emerging constraints method, warming in the eastern Pacific
is amplified, the reduction in the East-West SST gradient and the weakening of the trade winds are more marked
(Section 1.3.4.2).The application of this method by Ying et al. 2019 also suggests that the cold tongue bias
leads to an underestimation of future warming in the eastern Equatorial Pacific.
Limitations of emergent constraints and alternative strategies
Although useful, the emerging constraints method has several important limitations.
The method is highly dependent on the quality and duration of available observations (Hall et al.
2019). It is based on statistical methods that may lack a robust physical foundation
(Caldwell et al. 2018). Moreover, it assumes stationarity of climatic mechanisms, a questionable
questionable in a rapidly changing climate (Eyring et al. 2019). Finally, the non-linear complexity of climate
climate interactions may exceed the predictive capabilities of simple linear correlations (Brient

1.3. The Tropical Pacific in climate models

[FIGURE 1.24] Precipitation bias correction in CMIP5 projections according to RCP8.5: (a) raw projections of RSST, precipitation (contours), and wind stress (vectors); (b) errors associated with precipitation biases in the western Pacific; (c) corrected projections. Source: Li et al. 2016. 2020). Faced with these limitations, several complementary approaches have been developed. A first strategy is to use simplified coupled models. For example, Seager et al. 2019 have developed a coupled ocean-atmosphere model based on observed mean states, thus minimizing the biases of CMIP models. Their work suggests that the El Niño-like warming pattern simulated over the recent period results largely from these mean-state biases. Once these are corrected, their simulations instead predict a strengthening of the equatorial zonal gradient, thus attributing this divergence to biases rather than internal variability.

1.3. The Tropical Pacific in climate models

Another promising approach is the pseudo-global warming (PGW) method, initially developed by Schär et al. 1996, then formalized by Kimura et al. 2007 and regularly used in the
scientific community (Sato et al. 2007; Trapp et al. 2021). This technique, currently applied to the
to the atmospheric component, consists in directly tackling the problem of mean-state biases, by
superimposing climate change anomalies derived from global models onto actual observed conditions.
conditions. This approach eliminates the influence of climate model biases on future atmospheric
future atmospheric projections. Dutheil et al. 2019 combine regional dynamic downscaling
(use of a higher-resolution regional atmospheric model forced at the boundaries by global models) with the PGW method, incorporating the emergent constraints approach, to correct
not only SST's current biases but also their future projections. Their results show that
that correcting for projected changes in SST leads to a considerable decrease in precipitation in the SPCZ region, a result radically different from that suggested by the uncorrected
CMIP5 models. Finally, some use data assimilation, integrating observational data into climate
into climate models to improve representations of current climate and, consequently, future
future projections (Meehl et al. 2014). However, it can induce artificial discontinuities
between historical periods and future scenarios (Fyfe et al. 2016), requiring methodological
methodological precautions.
So, just as the scientific community cannot agree on the future structuring of the
structuring of the Equatorial Pacific and the mechanisms involved, there is still no consensus
as to why climate models are struggling to reproduce the strengthening of the Walker cell
observed in recent decades (Coats et al. 2017; Bai et al. 2023). Interpretations vary between underestimated internal variability (Power et al. 2021), systematic model biases
(Seager et al. 2019), or misrepresentation of forcings (Takahashi et al. 2016; Kang
et al. 2023). This discrepancy highlights the persistent uncertainties surrounding the reliability of future
projections in the Tropical Pacific.

1.4. Thesis objectives

1.4
Thesis objectives
The Tropical Pacific occupies a central place in the global climate system as the main driver of the Walker circulation, a key element in tropical climatology. To understand
the future evolution of this region, in a context of anthropogenic climate change, is a major
is a major scientific and societal challenge.
Considerable progress has been made in recent decades in identifying the mechanisms
mechanisms responsible for the Tropical Pacific's response to anthropogenic forcing. However, to date
there is as yet no consensus on the relative contribution of these mechanisms to the spatial structuring
surface warming projected by climate models. This spatial structure is important to understand, as it determines changes in the vertical stability of the atmosphere, and hence in atmospheric and oceanic circulation.
atmospheric and oceanic circulation. Most climate models project El Niño-type warming, marked by a weakening of the zonal temperature gradient and trade winds.
trade winds. Differential evaporative damping is often identified as the main thermodynamic driver of this response. However, other studies highlight the role of atmospheric processes (Vecchi et al. 2007; Held et al. 2006) or coupled ocean-atmosphere mechanisms, such as the
Bjerknes feedback (Fu et al. 2023), in the weakening of the zonal temperature gradient and the Walker circulation.
Walker circulation.
Furthermore, future projections for the Tropical Pacific from climate models remain
tainted by significant uncertainties. However, observations over the last few decades show, on the contrary
on the contrary, show a La Niña-like response (strengthening of the zonal gradient of surface temperature
and trade winds), which does not appear to be simply attributable to internal climate variability (Watanabe et al.
al. 2024; Wills et al. 2022). However, such a response does not constitute the dominant trend in
climate model projections. This diversity of future climate changes in the Tropical Pacific
projected by the models is a major source of uncertainty. This uncertainty is reinforced by
biases in the representation of current climate in coupled models, likely to alter the relative importance
mechanisms controlling mean-state changes in the Tropical Pacific. To date, there is no clear consensus on the impact of these biases.
that a correction of the mean state would reinforce an El Niño-like response (Li et al. 2016), while others
others, on the contrary, envisage a La Niña-like response after correction (Seager et al.
2019).
Against this backdrop, our study proposes to establish a unified framework for quantitatively assessing
the relative contribution of each mechanism to the evolution of the equatorial zonal SST gradient, through
dedicated numerical experiments on several climate models. The aim of this thesis was to
to answer three main questions:
- (1) What are the physical mechanisms governing the spatial structure of warming
in the Tropical Pacific?
- (2) What is the origin of intermodel diversity in CMIP projections?
- (3) What influence do biases in the representation of current climate in coupled models have on future projections?
future projections?
In order to provide some answers to these questions, an original methodological approach has been developed, combining statistical analysis of the CMIP multi-model database and numerical simulations with correction for inter-model bias.
numerical simulations with mean-state bias correction.
The first part of the work is based on a statistical analysis of a set of
63 climate models from the CMIP5 and CMIP6 projects. This analysis is based on a

1.4. Objectives of the thesis

simplified heat balance of the oceanic mixing layer inspired by Zhang et al. 2014. It enables
identify and quantify the relative contributions of different physical processes (feedbacks,
air-sea heat fluxes, ocean dynamics) to the structuring of surface warming, as well as
identify the main factors responsible for the diversity of responses between models. The results
of this approach will be presented in Chapter 3.
In addition, an original strategy of forced ocean simulations has been implemented to
dissect the physical mechanisms of ocean response to climate change, and the dependence of this response on climate model biases. This approach involves correcting the mean oceanic state
by imposing a climatology of air-sea flows derived from atmospheric reanalysis, and therefore more
more realistic than that of climate models. Conceptually, this strategy is inspired by the
pseudo-global warming (PGW) method used for atmospheric simulations (Schär et al. 1996; Kimura
et al. 2007), but adapts it specifically to the oceanic context: rather than superimposing climate anomalies on observed meteorological conditions, we correct the mean oceanic state
by imposing air-sea fluxes derived from atmospheric reanalyses, thus isolating the oceanic response to climate change
to climate change, while overcoming the persistent biases of coupled models.
These corrected ocean simulations are then forced by changes in fluxes (heat, wind,
freshwater) simulated by different CMIP models, in order to assess the influence of these biases on future
of the Tropical Pacific. Specific sensitivity experiments are used to isolate the individual effects of each type of forcing
of each type of forcing (wind stress, freshwater and heat fluxes) on the oceanic response
response, in order to quantify the respective contribution of thermodynamic and dynamic processes
in the simulated ocean changes. This approach was initially applied to the IPSLCM6A-LR model, then extended to other models representative of the entire CMIP base, such as ECEarth3, CNRM-ESM2-1 or the multi-model mean. This makes it possible to assess the robustness of the results,
and to generalize them. The analyses for IPSL-CM6A-LR will be detailed in Chapter 4, while the
the generalization of the method to other models will be discussed in Chapter 5.
Finally, Chapter 6 provides a summary of the main results obtained, set in the wider context of
context of climate projections for the Tropical Pacific. This summary will highlight
the robustness of the proposed experimental strategy for correcting mean-state biases, and its relevance in
to improve our understanding of the mechanisms driving climate change in this strategic
strategic region. Beyond this synthesis, the chapter will open up new perspectives, in particular
through a preliminary study of the influence of physical bias correction on ocean biogeochemical
biogeochemical projections. Finally, the limitations of the approach adopted will be discussed,
as well as methodological avenues that could be explored in future work to further refine our
our understanding of the tropical climate system.

In this chapter, we present the set of methods and tools used to achieve the objectives of this thesis, which aims to understand the mechanisms governing future physical ocean projections in the Tropical Pacific. Our methodological approach is based on two complementary approaches: a multi-model statistical approach and a dynamic approach based on forced simulations. We begin by describing the CMIP databases in section 2.1, which form the basis of the multi-model statistical analysis developed in chapter 3. Section 2.2
then presents the NEMO-PISCES ocean model, used for the forced simulations analysed
in chapters 4 and 5. The following sections detail the specific methodologies developed for our study.
developed for our study. Section 2.3.2 describes the decomposition of heat fluxes into forcing and feedback components, essential for understanding ocean-atmosphere interactions. Sections ??
and 2.5 present, respectively, the statistical and dynamic approaches used to identify
mechanisms responsible for the spatial patterns of warming in the Tropical Pacific. These
two approaches, although different in concept, are complementary and help to mutually
validate each other's results.

The "historical" period is defined as the average of the years 1900-1925, and the "future" period as the average of the years 2075-2100. Future changes (∆) represent the difference between these two periods. Recent period changes (∆pres) are calculated as the difference between the 2000-2020 and 1980-2000 averages, enabling direct comparison with available observations. Historical climatology is derived from historical simulations and averaged over a 25-year period, from 1900 to 1925. For future projections, we use simulations from the Scenario Model Intercomparison Project, also averaged over a 25-year period, from 2075 to 2100. The scenarios considered are RCP8.5 for CMIP5 and SSP585 for CMIP6. The multi-model mean (MMM) corresponds to the arithmetic mean calculated over all 63 combined simulations of CMIP5 and CMIP6. After downloading, all model outputs were interpolated onto a common 1◦× 1◦ grid for ease of comparison. The SST observations used for model evaluation come from COBE data (Hirahara et al. 2014). To calculate the climatological SST bias, we determined the difference between model SST and COBE observations over the period 1900-1925. It should be noted that, for Chapters 4 and 5, the analyses performed on the multi-model mean are based on a restricted set of 53 models only (indicated by an asterisk in Table 2.2). This more limited selection is explained by the fact that the methodology applied in these chapters differs slightly from that of chapter 3, and requires an additional set of variables. However, some models do not provide all the necessary variables, such as specific humidity or integrated water vapour on the atmospheric column, which led to their exclusion from the analysis.

[TABLE 2.2] List of CMIP5 and CMIP6 models ∗Models selected for Chapters 4 and 5. 2.2 NEMO-PISCES model In Chapters 4 and 5, we use simulations of climate change forced by observations (methodology detailed in the following sections). These simulations are based on the IPSL-CM6A-LR, CNRM-ESM2-1 and EC-Earth3 climate models, all of which incorporate NEMO3.6 (Nucleus for European Modelling of the Ocean) as their general ocean circulation model Madec et al. 2016. NEMO3.6 has a horizontal resolution of one degree, which is refined to a third of a degree near the equator to better represent tropical dynamics. Its vertical discretization comprises 75 levels, with a fine resolution of one meter at the surface, progressively extending to 200 meters in the deep ocean, enabling detailed representation of the entire water column. The model's architecture integrates several interconnected components. The physical OPA (Océan PArallélisé) component forms the heart of the system. This is coupled to the LIM3 (Louvain-la-Neuve Ice Model) sea ice model developed by Rousset et al. 2015, as well as to the PISCES-v2 (Pelagic Interaction Scheme for Carbon and Ecosystem Studies) biogeochemical model developed by Aumont et al. 2015. This configuration enables integrated modeling of physical, glaciological and biogeochemical ocean processes. However, in some configurations40

2.2 NEMO-PISCES model

tions used for our study, the physical and biogeochemical components of the ocean operate
without including the sea ice component. This approach can lead to thermal
thermal anomalies, notably local sub-freezing temperatures in the polar regions.
polar regions. To solve this technical problem, we have adopted the solution proposed by
Silvy et al. 2022: the ocean temperature is artificially capped at the freezing point, both in the equation of state and in the model.
both in the equation of state and in the calculation of the Brunt-Väisälä frequency. This method ensures
physical consistency of the model even in the absence of an explicit sea ice representation.

2.3 Heat flux changes: calculation of the heat flux-dependent feedback component

SST
2.3
Heat flux changes: calculating the SST-dependent feedback component
2.3.1
General approach and methodological framework
The radiation balance at the ocean-atmosphere interface plays a crucial role in understanding and modelling climate change.
of climate change. As mentioned in Section 1.2.3, this balance comprises a downward flux
flux emitted by the ocean in response. To quantitatively analyze the evolution of these fluxes in a context of global warming, it is necessary to establish a diagnostic decomposition. Previous studies have shown that these flux changes,
noted ∆Q, were strongly influenced by SST (Held et al. 2006; Xie et al. 2010; Zhang et al. 2014).
In particular, variations in SST, also noted ∆T, can lead to much more significant changes in surface fluxes than direct radiative forcings induced by greenhouse gases
(Pendergrass et al. 2018; Shakespeare et al. 2022). The method we adopt, proposed
by Zhang et al. 2014, is based on separating two main components of heat flux changes
heat fluxes at the ocean-atmosphere interface: an SST-dependent component and an SST-independent component. As a reminder, the independent component, denoted ∆QFor, includes radiative
radiative forcings directly linked to atmospheric changes induced by the increasing
concentration of greenhouse gases. The SST-dependent component, ∆QFbk, includes flux changes directly
changes in fluxes directly influenced by changes in SST, including feedback effects
where ocean warming modifies atmospheric fluxes. ∆QFbk can be formulated as
∆QFbk = -α∆T, where α is the total feedback coefficient.
This gives:
∆Q = ∆QFor -α∆T
(2.1)
This distinction provides a better understanding of the feedback mechanisms in the climate system
climate system, facilitates the identification of external forcings and their direct impacts on heat fluxes,
and is essential for the correct interpretation of climate simulation results. The analytical
we develop in the following sections aims to quantify these two components for each of the
each of the flux terms, in order to assess their respective contribution to the projected changes in SST
in the Tropical Pacific.
2.3.2
Breakdown of feedback components
2.3.2.1
Latent heat flux feedback
As presented in the introduction (Section 1.2.3), the change in latent heat flux can be
decomposed into a wind speed-dependent part, independent of local changes in SST,
which is considered as a direct forcing, ∆LHFor, and into a part directed by the specific humidity gradient
between the ocean and the atmosphere, linearly dependent on local changes in SST, which
acts as a negative feedback, ∆LHFbk. This feedback can be derived as follows.
As a reminder, the global formula for surface latent heat flux (LH) can be expressed
